2x" width="180"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><i>At the house of the writing pig</i>.</div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption"><i>The Upside Downs of Little Lady Lovekins and Old Man Muffaroo</i>, comics by Gustave Verbeek containing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reversible_figure" title="Reversible figure">reversible figures</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ambigram" title="Ambigram">ambigram</a> sentences (March 1904).</div></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/British_comics" title="British comics">British comics</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_American_comics" title="History of American comics">History of American comics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/American_comic_book" title="American comic book">American comic book</a></div>
<p>Illustrated humour periodicals were popular in 19th-century Britain, the earliest of which was the short-lived <i><a href="/wiki/The_Glasgow_Looking_Glass" title="The Glasgow Looking Glass">The Glasgow Looking Glass</a></i> in 1825.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[11]</a></sup> The most popular was <i><a href="/wiki/Punch_(magazine)" title="Punch (magazine)">Punch</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClarkClark199117_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClarkClark199117-14">[12]</a></sup> which popularized the term <i>cartoon</i> for its humorous caricatures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarvey200177_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarvey200177-15">[13]</a></sup> On occasion the cartoons in these magazines appeared in sequences;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEClarkClark199117_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEClarkClark199117-14">[12]</a></sup> the character <a href="/wiki/Ally_Sloper" title="Ally Sloper">Ally Sloper</a> featured in the earliest serialized comic strip when the character began to feature in its own weekly magazine in 1884.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeskinCook2012xxii_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeskinCook2012xxii-16">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>American comics developed out of such magazines as <i><a href="/wiki/Puck_(magazine)" title="Puck (magazine)">Puck</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Judge_(magazine)" title="Judge (magazine)">Judge</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Life_(magazine)" title="Life (magazine)">Life</a></i>. The success of illustrated humour supplements in the <i><a href="/wiki/New_York_World" title="New York World">New York World</a></i> and later the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_York_American" title="New York American">New York American</a></i>, particularly Outcault's <i>The Yellow Kid</i>, led to the development of newspaper comic strips. Early <a href="/wiki/Sunday_comics" title="Sunday comics">Sunday strips</a> were full-page<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENordling1995123_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENordling1995123-17">[15]</a></sup> and often in colour. Between 1896 and 1901 cartoonists experimented with sequentiality, movement, and speech balloons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGordon200235_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGordon200235-18">[16]</a></sup> An example is <a href="/wiki/Gustave_Verbeek" title="Gustave Verbeek">Gustave Verbeek</a>, who wrote his comic series "The UpsideDowns of Old Man Muffaroo and Little Lady Lovekins" between 1903 and 1905. These comics were made in such a way that one could read the 6-panel comic, flip the book and keep reading. He made 64 such comics in total. In 2012 a remake of a selection of the comics was made by Marcus Ivarsson in the book 'In Uppåner med Lilla Lisen &amp; Gamle Muppen'. (<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1067248974">.mw-parser-output cite.citation{font-style:inherit;word-wrap:break-word}.mw-parser-output .citation q{quotes:"\"""\"""'""'"}.mw-parser-output .citation:target{background-color:rgba(0,127,255,0.133)}.mw-parser-output .id-lock-free a,.mw-parser-output .citation .cs1-lock-free a{background:linear-gradient(transparent,transparent),url("//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/65/Lock-green.svg")right 0.1em center/9px no-repeat}.mw-parser-output .id-lock-limited a,.mw-parser-output .id-lock-registration a,.mw-parser-output .citation .cs1-lock-limited a,.mw-parser-output .citation .cs1-lock-registration a{background:linear-gradient(transparent,transparent),url("//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d6/Lock-gray-alt-2.svg")right 0.1em center/9px no-repeat}.mw-parser-output .id-lock-subscription a,.mw-parser-output .citation .cs1-lock-subscription a{background:linear-gradient(transparent,transparent),url("//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/aa/Lock-red-alt-2.svg")right 0.1em center/9px no-repeat}.mw-parser-output .cs1-ws-icon a{background:linear-gradient(transparent,transparent),url("//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg")right 0.1em center/12px no-repeat}.mw-parser-output .cs1-code{color:inherit;background:inherit;border:none;padding:inherit}.mw-parser-output .cs1-hidden-error{display:none;color:#d33}.mw-parser-output .cs1-visible-error{color:#d33}.mw-parser-output .cs1-maint{display:none;color:#3a3;margin-left:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .cs1-format{font-size:95%}.mw-parser-output .cs1-kern-left{padding-left:0.2em}.mw-parser-output .cs1-kern-right{padding-right:0.2em}.mw-parser-output .citation .mw-selflink{font-weight:inherit}</style><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-91-7089-524-1" title="Special:BookSources/978-91-7089-524-1">978-91-7089-524-1</a>)
</p>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto;overflow:hidden;width:auto;max-width:608px"><div class="thumbinner"><div class="noresize" style="overflow:auto"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mr._A._Mutt_Starts_in_to_Play_the_Races_1907.jpg" title="Bud Fisher's Mutt and Jeff (1907–1982) was the first successful daily comic strip (1907)."><img alt="Five-panel comic strip." data-file-height="544" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="204" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Mr._A._Mutt_Starts_in_to_Play_the_Races_1907.jpg/600px-Mr._A._Mutt_Starts_in_to_Play_the_Races_1907.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Mr._A._Mutt_Starts_in_to_Play_the_Races_1907.jpg/900px-Mr._A._Mutt_Starts_in_to_Play_the_Races_1907.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Mr._A._Mutt_Starts_in_to_Play_the_Races_1907.jpg/1200px-Mr._A._Mutt_Starts_in_to_Play_the_Races_1907.jpg 2x" width="600"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mr._A._Mutt_Starts_in_to_Play_the_Races_1907.jpg" title="File:Mr. A. Mutt Starts in to Play the Races 1907.jpg"> </a></div><a href="/wiki/Bud_Fisher" title="Bud Fisher">Bud Fisher</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Mutt_and_Jeff" title="Mutt and Jeff">Mutt and Jeff</a></i> (1907–1982) was the first successful daily comic strip (1907).</div></div></div>
<p>Shorter, black-and-white daily strips began to appear early in the 20th century, and became established in newspapers after the success in 1907 of <a href="/wiki/Bud_Fisher" title="Bud Fisher">Bud Fisher</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Mutt_and_Jeff" title="Mutt and Jeff">Mutt and Jeff</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarvey199411_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarvey199411-19">[17]</a></sup> In Britain, the <a href="/wiki/Amalgamated_Press" title="Amalgamated Press">Amalgamated Press</a> established a popular style of a sequence of images with text beneath them, including <i><a href="/wiki/Illustrated_Chips" title="Illustrated Chips">Illustrated Chips</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Comic_Cuts" title="Comic Cuts">Comic Cuts</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBramlettCookMeskin201645_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBramlettCookMeskin201645-20">[18]</a></sup> Humour strips predominated at first, and in the 1920s and 1930s strips with continuing stories in genres such as adventure and drama also became popular.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarvey199411_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarvey199411-19">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>Thin periodicals called <a href="/wiki/American_comic_book" title="American comic book">comic books</a> appeared in the 1930s, at first reprinting newspaper comic strips; by the end of the decade, original content began to dominate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERhoades20082_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERhoades20082-21">[19]</a></sup> The success in 1938 of <i><a href="/wiki/Action_Comics" title="Action Comics">Action Comics</a></i> and its lead hero <a href="/wiki/Superman" title="Superman">Superman</a> marked the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Age_of_Comic_Books" title="Golden Age of Comic Books">Golden Age of Comic Books</a>, in which the <a href="/wiki/Superhero_comics" title="Superhero comics">superhero genre</a> was prominent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERhoades2008x_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERhoades2008x-22">[20]</a></sup> In the UK and the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth</a>, the <a href="/wiki/DC_Thomson" title="DC Thomson">DC Thomson</a>-created <i><a href="/wiki/The_Dandy" title="The Dandy">Dandy</a></i> (1937) and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Beano" title="The Beano">Beano</a></i> (1938) became successful humor-based titles, with a combined circulation of over 2 million copies by the 1950s. Their characters, including "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dennis_the_Menace_(UK)" title="Dennis the Menace (UK)">Dennis the Menace</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Desperate_Dan" title="Desperate Dan">Desperate Dan</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/The_Bash_Street_Kids" title="The Bash Street Kids">The Bash Street Kids</a>" have been read by generations of British children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChildsStorry2013532_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChildsStorry2013532-23">[21]</a></sup> The comics originally experimented with superheroes and action stories before settling on humorous strips featuring a mix of the Amalgamated Press and US comic book styles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBramlettCookMeskin201646_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBramlettCookMeskin201646-24">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WonderworldComics3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1699" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="241" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/WonderworldComics3.jpg/170px-WonderworldComics3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/WonderworldComics3.jpg/255px-WonderworldComics3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/WonderworldComics3.jpg/340px-WonderworldComics3.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WonderworldComics3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Superhero_comics" title="Superhero comics">Superheroes</a> have been a staple of <a href="/wiki/American_comic_book" title="American comic book">American comic books</a> (<i>Wonderworld Comics</i> <abbr title="Number">#</abbr>3, 1939; cover: <a href="/wiki/Flame_(comics)" title="Flame (comics)">The Flame</a> by <a href="/wiki/Will_Eisner" title="Will Eisner">Will Eisner</a>).</div></div></div>
<p>The popularity of superhero comic books declined following World War II,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGabilliet201051_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGabilliet201051-25">[23]</a></sup> while comic book sales continued to increase as other genres proliferated, such as <a href="/wiki/Romance_comics" title="Romance comics">romance</a>, <a href="/wiki/Western_comics" title="Western comics">westerns</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crime_comics" title="Crime comics">crime</a>, <a href="/wiki/Horror_comics" title="Horror comics">horror</a>, and humour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGabilliet201049_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGabilliet201049-26">[24]</a></sup> Following a sales peak in the early 1950s, the content of comic books (particularly crime and horror) was subjected to scrutiny from parent groups and government agencies, which culminated in <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate_Subcommittee_on_Juvenile_Delinquency" title="United States Senate Subcommittee on Juvenile Delinquency">Senate hearings</a> that led to the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Comics_Code_Authority" title="Comics Code Authority">Comics Code Authority</a> self-censoring body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGabilliet201049–50_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGabilliet201049–50-27">[25]</a></sup> The Code has been blamed for stunting the growth of American comics and maintaining its low status in American society for much of the remainder of the century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGabilliet201050_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGabilliet201050-28">[26]</a></sup> Superheroes re-established themselves as the most prominent comic book genre by the early 1960s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGabilliet201052–55_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGabilliet201052–55-29">[27]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Underground_comix" title="Underground comix">Underground comix</a> challenged the Code and readers with adult, countercultural content in the late 1960s and early 1970s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGabilliet201066_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGabilliet201066-30">[28]</a></sup> The underground gave birth to the <a href="/wiki/Alternative_comics" title="Alternative comics">alternative comics</a> movement in the 1980s and its mature, often experimental content in non-superhero genres.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHatfield200520,_26Lopes2009123Rhoades2008140_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHatfield200520,_26Lopes2009123Rhoades2008140-31">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>Comics in the US has had a <a href="/wiki/Low_culture" title="Low culture">lowbrow</a> reputation stemming from its roots in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mass_culture" title="Mass culture">mass culture</a>; cultural elites sometimes saw popular culture as threatening culture and society. In the latter half of the 20th century, popular culture won greater acceptance, and the lines between high and low culture began to blur. Comics nevertheless continued to be stigmatized, as the medium was seen as entertainment for children and illiterates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELopes2009xx–xxi_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELopes2009xx–xxi-32">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Graphic_novel" title="Graphic novel">graphic novel</a>—book-length comics—began to gain attention after <a href="/wiki/Will_Eisner" title="Will Eisner">Will Eisner</a> popularized the term with his book <i><a href="/wiki/A_Contract_with_God" title="A Contract with God">A Contract with God</a></i> (1978).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPetersen2010222_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPetersen2010222-33">[31]</a></sup> The term became widely known with the public after the commercial success of <i><a href="/wiki/Maus" title="Maus">Maus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Watchmen" title="Watchmen">Watchmen</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Dark_Knight_Returns" title="The Dark Knight Returns">The Dark Knight Returns</a></i> in the mid-1980s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaplan2008172Sabin1993246Stringer1996262AhrensMeteling20101WilliamsLyons20107_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaplan2008172Sabin1993246Stringer1996262AhrensMeteling20101WilliamsLyons20107-34">[32]</a></sup> In the 21st century graphic novels became established in mainstream bookstores<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGabilliet2010210–211_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGabilliet2010210–211-35">[33]</a></sup> and libraries<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELopes2009151–152_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELopes2009151–152-36">[34]</a></sup> and webcomics became common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThorne2010209_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThorne2010209-37">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Franco-Belgian_and_European_comics">Franco-Belgian and European comics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Franco-Belgian and European comics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/European_comics" title="European comics">European comics</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Franco-Belgian_comics" title="Franco-Belgian comics">Franco-Belgian comics</a></div>
<p>The francophone Swiss <a href="/wiki/Rodolphe_T%C3%B6pffer" title="Rodolphe Töpffer">Rodolphe Töpffer</a> produced comic strips beginning in 1827,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGabilliet2010xiv_8-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGabilliet2010xiv-8">[7]</a></sup> and published theories behind the form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarvey2010_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarvey2010-38">[36]</a></sup> Cartoons appeared widely in newspapers and magazines from the 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELefèvre2010186_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELefèvre2010186-39">[37]</a></sup> The success of <i><a href="/wiki/Zig_et_Puce" title="Zig et Puce">Zig et Puce</a></i> in 1925 popularized the use of speech balloons in European comics, after which Franco-Belgian comics began to dominate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVessels201045Miller200717_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVessels201045Miller200717-40">[38]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Adventures_of_Tintin" title="The Adventures of Tintin">The Adventures of Tintin</a></i>, with its signature <a href="/wiki/Ligne_claire" title="Ligne claire">clear line</a> style,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScreech200527Miller200718_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScreech200527Miller200718-41">[39]</a></sup> was first serialized in newspaper comics supplements beginning in 1929,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller200717_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller200717-42">[40]</a></sup> and became an icon of Franco-Belgian comics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETheobald200482Screech200548McKinney20113_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETheobald200482Screech200548McKinney20113-43">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>Following the success of <span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr"><a href="/wiki/Le_Journal_de_Mickey" title="Le Journal de Mickey">Le Journal de Mickey</a></i></span> (est. 1934),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrove200576–78_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrove200576–78-44">[42]</a></sup> dedicated comics magazines<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPetersen2010214–215Lefèvre2010186_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPetersen2010214–215Lefèvre2010186-45">[43]</a></sup> like <i><a href="/wiki/Spirou_(magazine)" title="Spirou (magazine)">Spirou</a></i> (est. 1938) and <i><a href="/wiki/Tintin_(magazine)" title="Tintin (magazine)">Tintin</a></i> (1946-1993), and full-colour comic albums became the primary outlet for comics in the mid-20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPetersen2010214–215_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPetersen2010214–215-46">[44]</a></sup> As in the US, at the time comics were seen as infantile and a threat to culture and literacy; commentators stated that "none bear up to the slightest serious analysis",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[c]</a></sup> and that comics were "the sabotage of all art and all literature".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrove200545–46_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrove200545–46-49">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[d]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1960s, the term <i><span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">bandes dessinées</i></span></i> ("drawn strips") came into wide use in French to denote the medium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrove200551_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrove200551-51">[47]</a></sup> Cartoonists began creating comics for mature audiences,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller1998116Lefèvre2010186_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller1998116Lefèvre2010186-52">[48]</a></sup> and the term "Ninth Art"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[e]</a></sup> was coined, as comics began to attract public and academic attention as an artform.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller200723_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller200723-54">[49]</a></sup> A group including <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Goscinny" title="René Goscinny">René Goscinny</a> and <a href="/wiki/Albert_Uderzo" title="Albert Uderzo">Albert Uderzo</a> founded the magazine <i><a href="/wiki/Pilote" title="Pilote">Pilote</a></i> in 1959 to give artists greater freedom over their work. Goscinny and Uderzo's <i><a href="/wiki/Asterix" title="Asterix">The Adventures of Asterix</a></i> appeared in it<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller200721_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller200721-55">[50]</a></sup> and went on to become the best-selling French-language comics series.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScreech2005204_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScreech2005204-56">[51]</a></sup> From 1960, the satirical and taboo-breaking <i><a href="/wiki/Hara-Kiri_(magazine)" title="Hara-Kiri (magazine)">Hara-Kiri</a></i> defied censorship laws in the countercultural spirit that led to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/May_1968_events_in_France" title="May 1968 events in France">May 1968 events</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller200722_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller200722-57">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>Frustration with censorship and editorial interference led to a group of <i>Pilote</i> cartoonists to found the adults-only <i><a href="/wiki/L%27%C3%89cho_des_savanes" title="L'Écho des savanes">L'Écho des savanes</a></i> in 1972. Adult-oriented and experimental comics flourished in the 1970s, such as in the experimental science fiction of <a href="/wiki/Jean_Giraud" title="Jean Giraud">Mœbius</a> and others in <i><a href="/wiki/M%C3%A9tal_hurlant" title="Métal hurlant">Métal hurlant</a></i>, even mainstream publishers took to publishing prestige-format <a href="/wiki/Adult_comics" title="Adult comics">adult comics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller200725–28_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller200725–28-58">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>From the 1980s, mainstream sensibilities were reasserted and serialization became less common as the number of comics magazines decreased and many comics began to be published directly as albums.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller200733–34_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller200733–34-59">[54]</a></sup> Smaller publishers such as <a href="/wiki/L%27Association" title="L'Association">L'Association</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeaty20079_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeaty20079-60">[55]</a></sup> that published longer works<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELefèvre2010189–190_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELefèvre2010189–190-61">[56]</a></sup> in non-traditional formats<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrove2005153_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrove2005153-62">[57]</a></sup> by <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Auteur_theory" title="Auteur theory">auteur</a></i>-istic creators also became common. Since the 1990s, mergers resulted in fewer large publishers, while smaller publishers proliferated. Sales overall continued to grow despite the trend towards a shrinking print market.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller200749–53_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller200749–53-63">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Japanese_comics">Japanese comics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Japanese comics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_manga" title="History of manga">History of manga</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manga" title="Manga">Manga</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tagosaku_to_Mokube_no_Tokyo_Kenbutsu.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="853" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="313" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Tagosaku_to_Mokube_no_Tokyo_Kenbutsu.jpg/220px-Tagosaku_to_Mokube_no_Tokyo_Kenbutsu.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Tagosaku_to_Mokube_no_Tokyo_Kenbutsu.jpg/330px-Tagosaku_to_Mokube_no_Tokyo_Kenbutsu.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Tagosaku_to_Mokube_no_Tokyo_Kenbutsu.jpg/440px-Tagosaku_to_Mokube_no_Tokyo_Kenbutsu.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tagosaku_to_Mokube_no_Tokyo_Kenbutsu.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rakuten_Kitazawa" title="Rakuten Kitazawa">Rakuten Kitazawa</a> created the first modern Japanese comic strip. (<i>Tagosaku to Mokube no Tōkyō Kenbutsu</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[f]</a></sup> 1902)</div></div></div>
<p>Japanese comics and cartooning (<i><span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Manga" title="Manga">manga</a></i></span></i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[g]</a></sup> have a history that has been seen as far back as the anthropomorphic characters in the 12th-to-13th-century <i><span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Ch%C5%8Dj%C5%AB-jinbutsu-giga" title="Chōjū-jinbutsu-giga">Chōjū-jinbutsu-giga</a></i></span></i>, 17th-century <i><span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Toba-e" title="Toba-e">toba-e</a></i></span></i> and <i><span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Kiby%C5%8Dshi" title="Kibyōshi">kibyōshi</a></i></span></i> picture books,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchodt199622_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchodt199622-69">[62]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Woodblock_printing" title="Woodblock printing">woodblock prints</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Ukiyo-e" title="Ukiyo-e">ukiyo-e</a> which were popular between the 17th and 20th centuries. The <i><span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">kibyōshi</i></span></i> contained examples of sequential images, movement lines,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMansfield2009253_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMansfield2009253-70">[63]</a></sup> and sound effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPetersen201042_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPetersen201042-71">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>Illustrated magazines for Western expatriates introduced Western-style satirical cartoons to Japan in the late 19th century. New publications in both the Western and Japanese styles became popular, and at the end of the 1890s, American-style newspaper comics supplements began to appear in Japan,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJohnson-Woods201021–22_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJohnson-Woods201021–22-72">[65]</a></sup> as well as some American comic strips.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchodt199622_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchodt199622-69">[62]</a></sup> 1900 saw the debut of the <i>Jiji Manga</i> in the <i>Jiji Shinpō</i> newspaper—the first use of the word "manga" in its modern sense,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJohnson-Woods201022_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJohnson-Woods201022-67">[61]</a></sup> and where, in 1902, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rakuten_Kitazawa" title="Rakuten Kitazawa">Rakuten Kitazawa</a> began the first modern Japanese comic strip.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPetersen2010128Gravett200421_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPetersen2010128Gravett200421-73">[66]</a></sup> By the 1930s, comic strips were serialized in large-circulation monthly girls' and boys' magazine and collected into hardback volumes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchodt199622Johnson-Woods201023–24_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchodt199622Johnson-Woods201023–24-74">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>The modern era of comics in Japan began after World War II, propelled by the success of the serialized comics of the prolific <a href="/wiki/Osamu_Tezuka" title="Osamu Tezuka">Osamu Tezuka</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGravett200424_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGravett200424-75">[68]</a></sup> and the comic strip <i><a href="/wiki/Sazae-san" title="Sazae-san">Sazae-san</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacWilliams20083HashimotoTraphagan200821Sugimoto2010255Gravett20048_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacWilliams20083HashimotoTraphagan200821Sugimoto2010255Gravett20048-76">[69]</a></sup> Genres and audiences diversified over the following decades. Stories are usually first serialized in magazines which are often hundreds of pages thick and may contain over a dozen stories;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchodt199623Gravett200413–14_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchodt199623Gravett200413–14-77">[70]</a></sup> they are later compiled in <i><span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Tank%C5%8Dbon" title="Tankōbon">tankōbon</a></i></span></i>-format books.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGravett200414_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGravett200414-78">[71]</a></sup> At the turn of the 20th and 21st centuries, nearly a quarter of all printed material in Japan was comics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrenner200713Lopes2009152Raz1999162Jenkins2004121_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrenner200713Lopes2009152Raz1999162Jenkins2004121-79">[72]</a></sup> Translations became extremely popular in foreign markets—in some cases equaling or surpassing the sales of domestic comics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELee2010158_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELee2010158-80">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Forms_and_formats">Forms and formats</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Forms and formats">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Comic_strip" title="Comic strip">Comic strips</a> are generally short, multipanel comics that traditionally most commonly appeared in newspapers. In the US, daily strips have normally occupied a single tier, while <a href="/wiki/Sunday_comics" title="Sunday comics">Sunday strips</a> have been given multiple tiers. In the early 20th century, daily strips were typically in black-and-white and Sundays were usually in colour and often occupied a full page.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBooker2014xxvi–xxvii_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBooker2014xxvi–xxvii-81">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>Specialized comics periodicals formats vary greatly in different cultures. <a href="/wiki/Comic_book" title="Comic book">Comic books</a>, primarily an American format, are thin periodicals<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOrr200811Collins2010227_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOrr200811Collins2010227-82">[75]</a></sup> usually published in colour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOrr200810_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOrr200810-83">[76]</a></sup> European and Japanese comics are frequently serialized in magazines—monthly or weekly in Europe,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJohnson-Woods201022_67-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJohnson-Woods201022-67">[61]</a></sup> and usually black-and-white and weekly in Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchodt199623Orr200810_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchodt199623Orr200810-84">[77]</a></sup> Japanese comics magazine typically run to hundreds of pages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchodt199623_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchodt199623-85">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto;overflow:hidden;width:auto;max-width:608px"><div class="thumbinner"><div class="noresize" style="overflow:auto"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Comics_volumes_-_international_comparison.jpg" title="A comparison of book formats for comics around the world. The left group is from Japan and shows the tankōbon and the smaller bunkobon formats. Those in the middle group of Franco-Belgian comics are in the standard A4-size comic album format. The right group of graphic novels is from English-speaking countries, where there is no standard format."><img alt="" data-file-height="1004" data-file-width="2748" decoding="async" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Comics_volumes_-_international_comparison.jpg/600px-Comics_volumes_-_international_comparison.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Comics_volumes_-_international_comparison.jpg/900px-Comics_volumes_-_international_comparison.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Comics_volumes_-_international_comparison.jpg/1200px-Comics_volumes_-_international_comparison.jpg 2x" width="600"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Comics_volumes_-_international_comparison.jpg" title="File:Comics volumes - international comparison.jpg"> </a></div>A comparison of book formats for comics around the world.  The left group is from Japan and shows the <i><span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Tank%C5%8Dbon" title="Tankōbon">tankōbon</a></i></span></i> and the smaller <i><span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Bunkobon" title="Bunkobon">bunkobon</a></i></span></i> formats. Those in the middle group of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Franco-Belgian_comics" title="Franco-Belgian comics">Franco-Belgian comics</a> are in the standard <a href="/wiki/ISO_216#A4_series" title="ISO 216">A4-size</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comic_album" title="Comic album">comic album</a> format. The right group of <a href="/wiki/Graphic_novel" title="Graphic novel">graphic novels</a> is from English-speaking countries, where there is no standard format.</div></div></div>
<p>Book-length comics take different forms in different cultures. European <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comic_albums" title="Comic albums">comic albums</a> are most commonly printed in <a href="/wiki/ISO_216#A4_series" title="ISO 216">A4-size</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrove201024McKinney2011_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrove201024McKinney2011-86">[79]</a></sup> colour volumes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPetersen2010214–215_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPetersen2010214–215-46">[44]</a></sup> In English-speaking countries, the <a href="/wiki/Trade_paperback_(comics)" title="Trade paperback (comics)">trade paperback</a> format originating from collected comic books have also been chosen for original material. Otherwise, bound volumes of comics are called graphic novels and are available in various formats. Despite incorporating the term "novel"—a term normally associated with fiction—"graphic novel" also refers to non-fiction and collections of short works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldsmith200516KarpKress20114–6_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldsmith200516KarpKress20114–6-87">[80]</a></sup> Japanese comics are collected in volumes called <i><a href="/wiki/Tank%C5%8Dbon" title="Tankōbon">tankōbon</a></i> following magazine serialization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPoitras200166–67_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPoitras200166–67-88">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Gag_cartoon" title="Gag cartoon">Gag</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Editorial_cartoon" title="Editorial cartoon">editorial cartoons</a> usually consist of a single panel, often incorporating a caption or speech balloon. Definitions of comics which emphasize sequence usually exclude gag, editorial, and other single-panel cartoons; they can be included in definitions that emphasize the combination of word and image.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarvey200176_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarvey200176-89">[82]</a></sup> Gag cartoons first began to proliferate in <a href="/wiki/Broadsheet" title="Broadsheet">broadsheets</a> published in Europe in the 18th and 19th centuries, and the term "cartoon"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[h]</a></sup> was first used to describe them in 1843 in the British humour magazine <i><a href="/wiki/Punch_(magazine)" title="Punch (magazine)">Punch</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarvey200177_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarvey200177-15">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Webcomic" title="Webcomic">Webcomics</a> are comics that are available on the internet. They are able to reach large audiences, and new readers usually can access archived installments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPetersen2010234–236_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPetersen2010234–236-91">[83]</a></sup> Webcomics can make use of an <a href="/wiki/Infinite_canvas" title="Infinite canvas">infinite canvas</a>—meaning they are not constrained by size or dimensions of a page.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPetersen2010234McCloud2000222_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPetersen2010234McCloud2000222-92">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some consider <a href="/wiki/Storyboard" title="Storyboard">storyboards</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERhoades200838_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERhoades200838-93">[85]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Wordless_novel" title="Wordless novel">wordless novels</a> to be comics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeronä2008225_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeronä2008225-94">[86]</a></sup> Film studios, especially in animation, often use sequences of images as guides for film sequences. These storyboards are not intended as an end product and are rarely seen by the public.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERhoades200838_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERhoades200838-93">[85]</a></sup> Wordless novels are books which use sequences of captionless images to deliver a narrative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECohen1977181_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECohen1977181-95">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Comics_studies">Comics studies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Comics studies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Comics_studies" title="Comics studies">Comics studies</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1062260506">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%;max-width:100%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:.5em 1.4em .8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:.5em 0 .8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{overflow:hidden;position:relative;margin:.5em auto .8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft span,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright span{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox>blockquote{margin:0;padding:0;border-left:0;font-family:inherit;font-size:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:110%;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote>:first-child{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote:last-child>:last-child{margin-bottom:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" “ ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" ” ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quote-title,.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quotebox-quote{display:block}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:640px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{width:100%!important;margin:0 0 .8em!important;float:none!important}}</style><div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:30em; ;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p>"Comics ... are sometimes four-legged and sometimes two-legged and sometimes fly and sometimes don't ... to employ a metaphor as mixed as the medium itself, defining comics entails cutting a Gordian-knotted enigma wrapped in a mystery ..."
</p>
</blockquote>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style=""><a href="/wiki/R._C._Harvey" title="R. C. Harvey">R. C. Harvey</a>, 2001<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarvey200176_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarvey200176-89">[82]</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>Similar to the problems of defining literature and film,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012128–129_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012128–129-96">[88]</a></sup> no consensus has been reached on a definition of the comics medium,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012124_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012124-97">[89]</a></sup> and attempted definitions and descriptions have fallen prey to numerous exceptions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012126_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012126-98">[90]</a></sup> Theorists such as Töpffer,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThomas2010158_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThomas2010158-99">[91]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/R.C._Harvey" title="R.C. Harvey">R.C. Harvey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Will_Eisner" title="Will Eisner">Will Eisner</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeaty201265_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeaty201265-100">[92]</a></sup> David Carrier,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012126,_131_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012126,_131-101">[93]</a></sup> Alain Rey,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012124_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012124-97">[89]</a></sup> and Lawrence Grove emphasize the combination of text and images,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrove201017–19_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrove201017–19-102">[94]</a></sup> though there are prominent examples of pantomime comics throughout its history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012126_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012126-98">[90]</a></sup> Other critics, such as Thierry Groensteen<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrove201017–19_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrove201017–19-102">[94]</a></sup> and Scott McCloud, have emphasized the primacy of sequences of images.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThomas2010157,_170_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThomas2010157,_170-103">[95]</a></sup> Towards the close of the 20th century, different cultures' discoveries of each other's comics traditions, the rediscovery of forgotten early comics forms, and the rise of new forms made defining comics a more complicated task.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012a112–113_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012a112–113-104">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p>European comics studies began with Töpffer's theories of his own work in the 1840s, which emphasized panel transitions and the visual–verbal combination. No further progress was made until the 1970s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiller2007101_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller2007101-105">[97]</a></sup> Pierre Fresnault-Deruelle then took a <a href="/wiki/Semiotics" title="Semiotics">semiotics</a> approach to the study of comics, analyzing text–image relations, page-level image relations, and image discontinuities, or what Scott McCloud later dubbed "closure".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012a112_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012a112-106">[98]</a></sup> In 1987, Henri Vanlier introduced the term <i><span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">multicadre</i></span></i>, or "multiframe", to refer to the comics page as a semantic unit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012a113_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012a113-107">[99]</a></sup> By the 1990s, theorists such as <a href="/wiki/Beno%C3%AEt_Peeters" title="Benoît Peeters">Benoît Peeters</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thierry_Groensteen" title="Thierry Groensteen">Thierry Groensteen</a> turned attention to artists' <a href="/wiki/Poiesis" title="Poiesis">poïetic</a> creative choices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012a112_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012a112-106">[98]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Thierry_Smolderen" title="Thierry Smolderen">Thierry Smolderen</a> and Harry Morgan have held relativistic views of the definition of comics, a medium that has taken various, equally valid forms over its history. Morgan sees comics as a subset of "<i><span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">les littératures dessinées</i></span></i>" (or "drawn literatures").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012a112–113_104-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012a112–113-104">[96]</a></sup> French theory has come to give special attention to the page, in distinction from American theories such as McCloud's which focus on panel-to-panel transitions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012a113_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012a113-107">[99]</a></sup> In the mid-2000s, <a href="/wiki/Neil_Cohn" title="Neil Cohn">Neil Cohn</a> began analyzing how comics are understood using tools from cognitive science, extending beyond theory by using actual psychological and neuroscience experiments. This work has argued that sequential images and page layouts both use separate rule-bound "grammars" to be understood that extend beyond panel-to-panel transitions and categorical distinctions of types of layouts, and that the brain's comprehension of comics is similar to comprehending other domains, such as language and music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECohn2013_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECohn2013-108">[100]</a></sup>
</p><p>Historical narratives of <i>manga</i> tend to focus either on its recent, post-WWII history, or on attempts to demonstrate deep roots in the past, such as to the <i><span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">Chōjū-jinbutsu-giga</i></span></i> picture scroll of the 12th and 13th centuries, or the early 19th-century <i>Hokusai Manga</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStewart201428–29_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStewart201428–29-109">[101]</a></sup> The first historical overview of Japanese comics was Seiki Hosokibara's <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">Nihon Manga-Shi</i></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[i]</a></sup> in 1924.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJohnson-Woods201023Stewart201429_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJohnson-Woods201023Stewart201429-111">[102]</a></sup> Early post-war Japanese criticism was mostly of a left-wing political nature until the 1986 publication of Tomofusa Kure's <i>Modern Manga: The Complete Picture</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[j]</a></sup> which de-emphasized politics in favour of formal aspects, such as structure and a "grammar" of comics. The field of <span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">manga</i></span> studies increased rapidly, with numerous books on the subject appearing in the 1990s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinsella200096–97_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinsella200096–97-112">[103]</a></sup> Formal theories of <i><span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">manga</i></span></i> have focused on developing a "manga expression theory",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[k]</a></sup> with emphasis on spatial relationships in the structure of images on the page, distinguishing the medium from film or literature, in which the flow of time is the basic organizing element.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinsella2000100_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinsella2000100-114">[104]</a></sup> Comics studies courses have proliferated at Japanese universities, and Japan Society for Studies in Cartoon and Comics <span style="font-size:85%;">(<a class="extiw" href="https://ja.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E6%97%A5%E6%9C%AC%E3%83%9E%E3%83%B3%E3%82%AC%E5%AD%A6%E4%BC%9A" title="ja:日本マンガ学会">ja</a>)</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[l]</a></sup> was established in 2001 to promote comics scholarship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorita201037–38_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorita201037–38-117">[105]</a></sup> The publication of <a href="/wiki/Frederik_L._Schodt" title="Frederik L. Schodt">Frederik L. Schodt</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Manga!_Manga!_The_World_of_Japanese_Comics" title="Manga! Manga! The World of Japanese Comics">Manga! Manga! The World of Japanese Comics</a></i> in 1983 led to the spread of use of the word <i>manga</i> outside Japan to mean "Japanese comics" or "Japanese-style comics".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStewart201430_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStewart201430-118">[106]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:292px;max-width:292px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:152px;max-width:152px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:200px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Will_Eisner_(San_Diego_Comic_Con,_2004).jpg"><img alt="An elderly bald man wearing glasses." data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Will_Eisner_%28San_Diego_Comic_Con%2C_2004%29.jpg/150px-Will_Eisner_%28San_Diego_Comic_Con%2C_2004%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Will_Eisner_%28San_Diego_Comic_Con%2C_2004%29.jpg/225px-Will_Eisner_%28San_Diego_Comic_Con%2C_2004%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Will_Eisner_%28San_Diego_Comic_Con%2C_2004%29.jpg/300px-Will_Eisner_%28San_Diego_Comic_Con%2C_2004%29.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:136px;max-width:136px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:200px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Scott_McCloud.Making_Comics_Tour.RISD.gk.JPG"><img alt="A middle-aged man seated behind a table, facing the camera." data-file-height="3072" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="201" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Scott_McCloud.Making_Comics_Tour.RISD.gk.JPG/134px-Scott_McCloud.Making_Comics_Tour.RISD.gk.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Scott_McCloud.Making_Comics_Tour.RISD.gk.JPG/201px-Scott_McCloud.Making_Comics_Tour.RISD.gk.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Scott_McCloud.Making_Comics_Tour.RISD.gk.JPG/268px-Scott_McCloud.Making_Comics_Tour.RISD.gk.JPG 2x" width="134"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption"><a href="/wiki/Will_Eisner" title="Will Eisner">Will Eisner</a> <i>(top)</i> and <a href="/wiki/Scott_McCloud" title="Scott McCloud">Scott McCloud</a> have proposed influential and controversial definitions of comics.</div></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Coulton_Waugh" title="Coulton Waugh">Coulton Waugh</a> attempted the first comprehensive history of American comics with <i>The Comics</i> (1947).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEInge1989214_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEInge1989214-119">[107]</a></sup> Will Eisner's <i><a href="/wiki/Comics_and_Sequential_Art" title="Comics and Sequential Art">Comics and Sequential Art</a></i> (1985) and <a href="/wiki/Scott_McCloud" title="Scott McCloud">Scott McCloud</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Understanding_Comics" title="Understanding Comics">Understanding Comics</a></i> (1993) were early attempts in English to formalize the study of comics. David Carrier's <i>The Aesthetics of Comics</i> (2000) was the first full-length treatment of comics from a philosophical perspective.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeskinCook2012xxix_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeskinCook2012xxix-120">[108]</a></sup> Prominent American attempts at definitions of comics include Eisner's, McCloud's, and Harvey's. Eisner described what he called "<a href="/wiki/Sequential_art" title="Sequential art">sequential art</a>" as "the arrangement of pictures or images and words to narrate a story or dramatize an idea";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYuan2011Eisner19855_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYuan2011Eisner19855-121">[109]</a></sup> Scott McCloud defined comics as "juxtaposed pictorial and other images in deliberate sequence, intended to convey information and/or to produce an aesthetic response in the viewer",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKovacsMarshall201110Holbo201213Harvey20101Beaty20126McCloud19939_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKovacsMarshall201110Holbo201213Harvey20101Beaty20126McCloud19939-122">[110]</a></sup> a strictly formal definition which detached comics from its historical and cultural trappings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeaty201267_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeaty201267-123">[111]</a></sup> R.C. Harvey defined comics as "pictorial narratives or expositions in which words (often lettered into the picture area within speech balloons) usually contribute to the meaning of the pictures and vice versa".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChute20107Harvey200176_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChute20107Harvey200176-124">[112]</a></sup> Each definition has had its detractors. Harvey saw McCloud's definition as excluding single-panel cartoons,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarvey20101_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarvey20101-125">[113]</a></sup> and objected to McCloud's de-emphasizing verbal elements, insisting "the essential characteristic of comics is the incorporation of verbal content".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012a113_107-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012a113-107">[99]</a></sup> Aaron Meskin saw McCloud's theories as an artificial attempt to legitimize the place of comics in art history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeaty201265_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeaty201265-100">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cross-cultural study of comics is complicated by the great difference in meaning and scope of the words for "comics" in different languages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorita201033_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorita201033-126">[114]</a></sup> The French term for comics, <i><span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">bandes dessinées</i></span></i> ("drawn strip") emphasizes the juxtaposition of drawn images as a defining factor,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012130Morita201033_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012130Morita201033-127">[115]</a></sup> which can imply the exclusion of even photographic comics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012130_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012130-128">[116]</a></sup> The term <i><span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">manga</i></span></i> is used in Japanese to indicate all forms of comics, cartooning,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJohnson-Woods2010336_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJohnson-Woods2010336-129">[117]</a></sup> and caricature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorita201033_126-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorita201033-126">[114]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Terminology">Terminology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Terminology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_comics_terminology" title="Glossary of comics terminology">Glossary of comics terminology</a></div>
<p>The term <i>comics</i> refers to the comics medium when used as an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uncountable_noun" title="Uncountable noun">uncountable noun</a> and thus takes the singular: "comics <i>is</i> a medium" rather than "comics <i>are</i> a medium". When <i>comic</i> appears as a countable noun it refers to instances of the medium, such as individual comic strips or comic books: "Tom's comics <i>are</i> in the basement."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChapman20128ChuteDeKoven2012175Fingeroth20084_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChapman20128ChuteDeKoven2012175Fingeroth20084-130">[118]</a></sup>
</p><p>Panels are individual images containing a segment of action,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELee197815_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELee197815-131">[119]</a></sup> often surrounded by a border.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEisner198528,_45_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEisner198528,_45-132">[120]</a></sup> Prime moments in a narrative are broken down into panels via a process called encapsulation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuncanSmith200910_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuncanSmith200910-133">[121]</a></sup> The reader puts the pieces together via the process of closure by using background knowledge and an understanding of panel relations to combine panels mentally into events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuncanSmith2009316_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuncanSmith2009316-134">[122]</a></sup> The size, shape, and arrangement of panels each affect the timing and pacing of the narrative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEisner198530_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEisner198530-135">[123]</a></sup> The contents of a panel may be asynchronous, with events depicted in the same image not necessarily occurring at the same time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuncanSmith2009315KarpKress201112–13_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuncanSmith2009315KarpKress201112–13-136">[124]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PunchandJudyComicsV01-0145-panel3.jpg"><img alt="A comics panel. In the top left, a caption with a yellow background reads, &quot;Suddenly the street is filled with angry people!&quot; In the main panel, anthropomorphic characters crowd a sidewalk. A monkey, standing to the left on the road beside the curb, says, &quot;Gosh! Where'd all these people come from?&quot; An overweight male on the sidewalk in the middle facing right says to a police officer, &quot;Hey! My watch disappeared from my parlor!&quot; A female near the bottom right, says to a male in the bottom right corner, &quot;My necklace! It's gone from the table!!&quot;" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="446" data-file-width="661" decoding="async" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/PunchandJudyComicsV01-0145-panel3.jpg/220px-PunchandJudyComicsV01-0145-panel3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/PunchandJudyComicsV01-0145-panel3.jpg/330px-PunchandJudyComicsV01-0145-panel3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/PunchandJudyComicsV01-0145-panel3.jpg/440px-PunchandJudyComicsV01-0145-panel3.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PunchandJudyComicsV01-0145-panel3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A caption (the yellow box) gives the narrator a voice. The characters' dialogue appears in <a href="/wiki/Speech_balloon" title="Speech balloon">speech balloons</a>. The tail of the balloon indicates the speaker.</div></div></div>
<p>Text is frequently incorporated into comics via <a href="/wiki/Speech_balloon" title="Speech balloon">speech balloons</a>, captions, and sound effects. Speech balloons indicate dialogue (or thought, in the case of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thought_balloon" title="Thought balloon">thought balloons</a>), with tails pointing at their respective speakers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELee197815Markstein2010Eisner1985157Dawson2010112Saraceni20039_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELee197815Markstein2010Eisner1985157Dawson2010112Saraceni20039-137">[125]</a></sup> Captions can give voice to a narrator, convey characters' dialogue or thoughts,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELee197815LygaLyga2004_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELee197815LygaLyga2004-138">[126]</a></sup> or indicate place or time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESaraceni20039KarpKress201118_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESaraceni20039KarpKress201118-139">[127]</a></sup> Speech balloons themselves are strongly associated with comics, such that the addition of one to an image is sufficient to turn the image into comics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEForcevilleVealeFeyaerts201056_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEForcevilleVealeFeyaerts201056-140">[128]</a></sup> Sound effects mimic non-vocal sounds textually using <a href="/wiki/Onomatopoeia" title="Onomatopoeia">onomatopoeia</a> sound-words.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuncanSmith2009156,_318_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuncanSmith2009156,_318-141">[129]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cartooning" title="Cartooning">Cartooning</a> is most frequently used in making comics, traditionally using ink (especially <a href="/wiki/India_ink" title="India ink">India ink</a>) with <a href="/wiki/Dip_pen" title="Dip pen">dip pens</a> or ink brushes;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarkstein2010LygaLyga2004161Lee1978145Rhoades2008139_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarkstein2010LygaLyga2004161Lee1978145Rhoades2008139-142">[130]</a></sup> mixed media and digital technology have become common. Cartooning techniques such as <a href="/wiki/Motion_lines" title="Motion lines">motion lines</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBramlett201225Guigar2010126Cates201098_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBramlett201225Guigar2010126Cates201098-143">[131]</a></sup> and abstract symbols are often employed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldsmith200521KarpKress201113–14_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldsmith200521KarpKress201113–14-144">[132]</a></sup>
</p><p>While comics are often the work of a single creator, the labour of making them is frequently divided between a number of specialists. There may be separate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comics_writer" title="Comics writer">writers</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comics_artist" title="Comics artist">artists</a>, and artists may specialize in parts of the artwork such as characters or backgrounds, as is common in Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEO'Nale2010384_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO'Nale2010384-145">[133]</a></sup> Particularly in American superhero comic books,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETondro201151_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETondro201151-146">[134]</a></sup> the art may be divided between a <a href="/wiki/Penciller" title="Penciller">penciller</a>, who lays out the artwork in pencil;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELygaLyga2004161_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELygaLyga2004161-147">[135]</a></sup> an <a href="/wiki/Inker" title="Inker">inker</a>, who finishes the artwork in ink;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarkstein2010LygaLyga2004161Lee1978145_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarkstein2010LygaLyga2004161Lee1978145-148">[136]</a></sup> a <a href="/wiki/Colorist" title="Colorist">colourist</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuncanSmith2009315_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuncanSmith2009315-149">[137]</a></sup> and a <a href="/wiki/Letterer" title="Letterer">letterer</a>, who adds the captions and speech balloons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELygaLyga2004163_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELygaLyga2004163-150">[138]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The English-language term <i>comics</i> derives from the humorous (or "<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/comic" title="wikt:comic">comic</a>") work which predominated in early American newspaper comic strips, but usage of the term has become standard for non-humorous works as well. The alternate spelling <i>comix</i> – coined by the <a href="/wiki/Underground_comix" title="Underground comix">underground comix</a> movement – is sometimes used to address this ambiguities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGomez_RomeroDahlman2012_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGomez_RomeroDahlman2012-151">[139]</a></sup> The term "comic book" has a similarly confusing history since they are most often not humorous and are periodicals, not regular books.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012p._131_(translator's_note)_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroensteen2012p._131_(translator's_note)-152">[140]</a></sup> It is common in English to refer to the comics of different cultures by the terms used in their languages, such as <i><span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Manga" title="Manga">manga</a></i></span></i> for Japanese comics, or <i><span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">bandes dessinées</i></span></i> for French-language <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Franco-Belgian_comics" title="Franco-Belgian comics">Franco-Belgian comics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcKinney2011xiii_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcKinney2011xiii-153">[141]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many cultures have taken their word for comics from English, including Russian (<span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">комикс</span></span>, <i><span title="Russian-language romanization"><i lang="ru-Latn"><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Russian_comics&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Russian comics (page does not exist)">komiks</a></i></span></i>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlaniz20107_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlaniz20107-154">[142]</a></sup> and German (<i><span title="German-language text"><i lang="de"><a href="/wiki/German_comics" title="German comics">Comic</a></i></span></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrahm2003_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrahm2003-155">[143]</a></sup> Similarly, the Chinese term <i><span title="Chinese-language romanization"><i lang="zh-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Manhua" title="Manhua">manhua</a></i></span></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWong200211Cooper-Chen2010177_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWong200211Cooper-Chen2010177-156">[144]</a></sup> and the Korean <i><span title="Korean-language romanization"><i lang="ko-Latn"><a href="/wiki/Manhwa" title="Manhwa">manhwa</a></i></span></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJohnson-Woods2010301_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJohnson-Woods2010301-157">[145]</a></sup> derive from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_character" title="Chinese character">Chinese characters</a> with which the Japanese term <i><span title="Japanese-language romanization"><i lang="ja-Latn">manga</i></span></i> is written.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper-Chen2010177Thompson2007xiii_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper-Chen2010177Thompson2007xiii-158">[146]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Comics&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-lower-alpha">
<div class="mw-references-wrap mw-references-columns"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-6">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">tankōbon<span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span title="Japanese-language text"><span lang="ja">単行本</span></span>, translation close to "independently appearing book")</span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-12">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">David Kunzle has compiled extensive collections of these and other proto-comics in his <i>The Early Comic Strip</i> (1973) and <i>The History of the Comic Strip</i> (1990).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBeaty201262_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBeaty201262-11">[10]</a></sup> </span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-48"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-48">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>: <i lang="fr">"... aucune ne supporte une analyse un peu serieuse."</i> – Jacqueline &amp; Raoul Dubois in <span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">La Presse enfantine française</i></span> (Midol, 1957)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrove200546_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrove200546-47">[45]</a></sup> </span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-50"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-50">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>: <i lang="fr">"C'est le sabotage de tout art et de toute littérature."</i> – Jean de Trignon in <span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">Histoires de la littérature enfantine de ma Mère l'Oye au Roi Babar</i></span> (<a href="/wiki/Hachette_(publisher)" title="Hachette (publisher)">Hachette</a>, 1950)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrove200546_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrove200546-47">[45]</a></sup> </span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-53"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-53">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>: <i lang="fr">neuvième art</i> </span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-64"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-64">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>Tagosaku and Mokube Sightseeing in Tokyo</i><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a>: <span lang="ja">田吾作と杢兵衛の東京見物</span>, <a href="/wiki/Hepburn_romanization" title="Hepburn romanization">Hepburn</a>: <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn"><i>Tagosaku to Mokube no Tokyo Kenbutsu</i></i></span>)</span> </span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-68"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-68">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>"Manga"</i><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a>: <span lang="ja">漫画</span>)</span> can be <a href="/wiki/Gloss_(annotation)" title="Gloss (annotation)">glossed</a> in many ways, amongst them "whimsical pictures", "disreputable pictures",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKarpKress201119_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKarpKress201119-65">[59]</a></sup> "irresponsible pictures",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGravett20049_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGravett20049-66">[60]</a></sup> "derisory pictures", and "sketches made for or out of a sudden inspiration".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJohnson-Woods201022_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJohnson-Woods201022-67">[61]</a></sup> </span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-90"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-90">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/cartoon" title="wikt:cartoon">cartoon</a>": from the Italian <span title="Italian-language text"><i lang="it">cartone</i></span>, meaning "card", which referred to the cardboard on which the cartoons were typically drawn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarvey200177_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarvey200177-15">[13]</a></sup> </span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-110"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-110">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFHosokibara1924">Hosokibara, Seiki (1924). <i>日本漫画史</i> [<i>Japanese Comics History</i>]. Yuzankaku.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=%E6%97%A5%E6%9C%AC%E6%BC%AB%E7%94%BB%E5%8F%B2&amp;rft.pub=Yuzankaku&amp;rft.date=1924&amp;rft.aulast=Hosokibara&amp;rft.aufirst=Seiki&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AComics"></span> </span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-113"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-113">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFKure1986">Kure, Tomofusa (1986). <i>現代漫画の全体像</i> [<i>Modern Manga: The Complete Picture</i>]. <li id="cite_note-115"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-115">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Manga expression theory"<span style="font-weight: normal"> (<a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a>: <span lang="ja">漫画表現論</span>, <a href="/wiki/Hepburn_romanization" title="Hepburn romanization">Hepburn</a>: <span title="Hepburn transliteration"><i lang="ja-Latn">manga hyōgenron</i></span>)</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKinsella2000100_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKinsella2000100-114">[104]</a></sup> </span>
